   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Aquaculture</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content"> 
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div> 
    <div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
      Jump to: 
     <a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 
     <a href="#p-search">search</a> 
    </div> 
    <div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr">
     <div class="thumb tmulti tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px"> 
       <div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:">
        Aquaculture
       </div> 
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px"> 
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Aquaculturechile.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Aquaculturechile.jpg/300px-Aquaculturechile.jpg" width="300" height="314" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9a/Aquaculturechile.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9a/Aquaculturechile.jpg 2x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="335"></a>
        </div> 
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"> 
         <center>
          Aquaculture installations in 
          <a href="/wiki/Zona_Sur" title="Zona Sur">southern Chile</a>
         </center> 
        </div> 
       </div> 
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px"> 
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Global_total_fish_harvest.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Global_total_fish_harvest.svg/300px-Global_total_fish_harvest.svg.png" width="300" height="196" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Global_total_fish_harvest.svg/450px-Global_total_fish_harvest.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Global_total_fish_harvest.svg/600px-Global_total_fish_harvest.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="490"></a>
        </div> 
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"> 
         <center>
          Global harvest of aquatic organisms in million tonnes, 1950–2010, as reported by the FAO 
          <sup id="cite_ref-faostat_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faostat-1">[1]</a></sup>
         </center> 
        </div> 
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p><b>Aquaculture</b>, also known as <b>aquafarming</b>, is the farming of aquatic organisms such as <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mollusc" title="Mollusc" class="mw-redirect">molluscs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_plant" title="Aquatic plant">aquatic plants</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-providence1_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-providence1-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Aquaculture involves cultivating freshwater and saltwater populations under controlled conditions, and can be contrasted with <a href="/wiki/Commercial_fishing" title="Commercial fishing">commercial fishing</a>, which is the harvesting of <a href="/wiki/Wild_fish" title="Wild fish" class="mw-redirect">wild fish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AmericanHeritageDef_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AmericanHeritageDef-4">[4]</a></sup> Broadly speaking, the relation of aquaculture to finfish and shellfish <a href="/wiki/Fisheries" title="Fisheries" class="mw-redirect">fisheries</a> is analogous to the relation of <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> to <a href="/wiki/Hunting_and_gathering_society" title="Hunting and gathering society" class="mw-redirect">hunting and gathering</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mariculture" title="Mariculture">Mariculture</a> refers to aquaculture practiced in marine environments and in underwater habitats.</p> 
     <p>According to the <a href="/wiki/FAO" title="FAO" class="mw-redirect">FAO</a>, aquaculture "is understood to mean the farming of aquatic organisms including fish, molluscs, crustaceans and aquatic plants. Farming implies some form of intervention in the rearing process to enhance production, such as regular stocking, feeding, protection from predators, etc. Farming also implies individual or corporate ownership of the stock being cultivated."<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The reported output from global aquaculture operations would supply one half of the fish and shellfish that is directly consumed by humans;<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> however, there are issues about the reliability of the reported figures.<sup id="cite_ref-overreporting_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-overreporting-8">[8]</a></sup> Further, in current aquaculture practice, products from several pounds of wild fish are used to produce one pound of a <a href="/wiki/Piscivorous" title="Piscivorous" class="mw-redirect">piscivorous</a> fish like <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture_of_salmon" title="Aquaculture of salmon" class="mw-redirect">salmon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Alliance_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alliance-9">[9]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Particular kinds of aquaculture include <a href="/wiki/Fish_farming" title="Fish farming">fish farming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shrimp_farm" title="Shrimp farm" class="mw-redirect">shrimp farming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oyster_farming" title="Oyster farming">oyster farming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mariculture" title="Mariculture">mariculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algaculture" title="Algaculture">algaculture</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Seaweed_farming" title="Seaweed farming">seaweed farming</a>), and the cultivation of <a href="/wiki/Ornamental_fish" title="Ornamental fish" class="mw-redirect">ornamental fish</a>. Particular methods include <a href="/wiki/Aquaponics" title="Aquaponics">aquaponics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Integrated_multi-trophic_aquaculture" title="Integrated multi-trophic aquaculture">integrated multi-trophic aquaculture</a>, both of which integrate fish farming and plant farming.</p> 
     <p></p>

     <p></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Delta_Pride_Catfish_farm_harvest.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photo of dripping, cup-shaped net, approximately 6 feet (1.8&nbsp;m) in diameter and equally tall, half full of fish, suspended from crane boom, with 4 workers on and around larger, ring-shaped structure in water" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Delta_Pride_Catfish_farm_harvest.jpg/220px-Delta_Pride_Catfish_farm_harvest.jpg" width="220" height="334" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Delta_Pride_Catfish_farm_harvest.jpg/330px-Delta_Pride_Catfish_farm_harvest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/41/Delta_Pride_Catfish_farm_harvest.jpg 2x" data-file-width="422" data-file-height="640"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Delta_Pride_Catfish_farm_harvest.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Workers harvest catfish from the Delta Pride Catfish farms in Mississippi
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The indigenous <a href="/wiki/Gunditjmara" title="Gunditjmara">Gunditjmara</a> people in <a href="/wiki/Victoria_(Australia)" title="Victoria (Australia)">Victoria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, may have raised eels as early as 6000&nbsp;BC. There is evidence that they developed about 100 square kilometres (39&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi) of volcanic <a href="/wiki/Floodplain" title="Floodplain">floodplains</a> in the vicinity of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Condah" title="Lake Condah" class="mw-redirect">Lake Condah</a> into a complex of channels and <a href="/wiki/Dam" title="Dam">dams</a>, and used woven <a href="/wiki/Fishing_trap" title="Fishing trap" class="mw-redirect">traps</a> to capture <a href="/wiki/Eel" title="Eel">eels</a>, and preserve them to eat all year round.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Aquaculture was operating in China circa 2500&nbsp;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> When the waters subsided after <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">river</a> <a href="/wiki/Flood" title="Flood">floods</a>, some fishes, mainly <a href="/wiki/Common_carp" title="Common carp">carp</a>, were trapped in <a href="/wiki/Lake" title="Lake">lakes</a>. Early aquaculturists fed their brood using <a href="/wiki/Nymph_(biology)" title="Nymph (biology)">nymphs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silkworm" title="Silkworm" class="mw-redirect">silkworm</a> feces, and ate them. A fortunate <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genetic</a> <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">mutation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Common_carp" title="Common carp">carp</a> led to the emergence of <a href="/wiki/Goldfish" title="Goldfish">goldfish</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" title="Tang Dynasty" class="mw-redirect">Tang Dynasty</a>.</p> 
     <p>Japanese cultivated <a href="/wiki/Seaweed" title="Seaweed">seaweed</a> by providing <a href="/wiki/Bamboo" title="Bamboo">bamboo</a> poles and, later, nets and <a href="/wiki/Oyster" title="Oyster">oyster</a> shells to serve as anchoring surfaces for <a href="/wiki/Spores" title="Spores" class="mw-redirect">spores</a>.</p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> bred fish in ponds and farmed oysters in coastal lagoons before 100 CE.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In central Europe, early Christian <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monasteries</a> adopted Roman aquacultural practices.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Aquaculture spread in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> since away from the seacoasts and the big rivers fish had to be salted so they did not rot.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Improvements in transportation during the 19th century made fresh fish easily available and inexpensive, even in inland areas, making aquaculture less popular. The 15th Century fishponds of the <a href="/wiki/T%C5%99ebo%C5%88" title="Třeboň">Trebon Basin</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> are maintained as a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site" title="UNESCO World Heritage Site" class="mw-redirect">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Hawaiians constructed oceanic <a href="/wiki/Fish_pond" title="Fish pond">fish ponds</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_aquaculture" title="Hawaiian aquaculture" class="mw-redirect">Hawaiian aquaculture</a>). A remarkable example is a fish pond dating from at least 1,000&nbsp;years ago, at Alekoko. Legend says that it was constructed by the mythical <a href="/wiki/Menehune" title="Menehune">Menehune</a> dwarf people.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In first half of 18th century German <a href="//de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stephan_Ludwig_Jacobi" class="extiw" title="de:Stephan Ludwig Jacobi">Stephan Ludwig Jacobi</a> experimented with <a href="/wiki/External_fertilization" title="External fertilization">external fertilization</a> of <a href="/wiki/Brown_trout" title="Brown trout">brown trouts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmons</a>. He wrote an article <i>"Von der künstlichen Erzeugung der Forellen und Lachse".</i> By the latter decades of the 18th Century, oyster farming had begun in estuaries along the Atlantic Coast of North America.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The word aquaculture appeared in an 1855 newspaper article in reference to the harvesting of ice.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> It also appeared in descriptions of the terrestrial agricultural practise of subirrigation in the late 19th century<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> before becoming associated primarily with the cultivation of aquatic plant and animal species.</p> 
     <p>In 1859, Stephen Ainsworth of <a href="/wiki/West_Bloomfield,_New_York" title="West Bloomfield, New York">West Bloomfield, New York</a>, began experiments with <a href="/wiki/Brook_trout" title="Brook trout">brook trout</a>. By 1864, Seth Green had established a commercial fish hatching operation at Caledonia Springs, near <a href="/wiki/Rochester,_New_York" title="Rochester, New York">Rochester, New York</a>. By 1866, with the involvement of Dr. W. W. Fletcher of <a href="/wiki/Concord,_Massachusetts" title="Concord, Massachusetts">Concord, Massachusetts</a>, artificial fish hatcheries were under way in both Canada and the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/Dildo_Island" title="Dildo Island">Dildo Island</a> fish hatchery opened in Newfoundland in 1889, it was the largest and most advanced in the world. The word aquaculture was used in descriptions of the hatcheries experiments with cod and lobster in 1890.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>By the 1920s, the American Fish Culture Company of <a href="/wiki/Carolina,_Rhode_Island" title="Carolina, Rhode Island">Carolina, Rhode Island</a> founded in the 1870s was one of the leading producers of trout. During the 1940s, they had perfected the method of manipulating the day and night cycle of fish so that they could be artificially spawned year around.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Californians harvested wild <a href="/wiki/Kelp" title="Kelp">kelp</a> and attempted to manage supply circa 1900, later labeling it a wartime resource.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p> 
     <div style="clear:both;"></div> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="21st-century_practice">21st-century practice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: 21st-century practice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <p>Harvest stagnation in <a href="/wiki/Wild_fisheries" title="Wild fisheries">wild fisheries</a> and <a href="/wiki/Overexploitation" title="Overexploitation">overexploitation</a> of popular marine species, combined with a growing demand for high quality protein, encouraged aquaculturists to domesticate other marine species.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-uscnews.usc.edu_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uscnews.usc.edu-26">[26]</a></sup> At the outset of modern aquaculture, many were optimistic that a "Blue Revolution" could take place in aquaculture, just as the <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a> of the 20th century had revolutionized agriculture.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Although land animals had long been domesticated, most seafood were still caught from the wild. Concerned about the impact of growing demand for seafood on the world's oceans, prominent ocean explorer <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Cousteau" title="Jacques Cousteau">Jacques Cousteau</a> wrote in 1973: "With earth’s burgeoning human populations to feed, we must turn to the sea with new understanding and new technology.”<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>About 430 (97%) of the <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> cultured as of 2007 were domesticated during the 20th and 21st centuries, of which an estimated 106 came in the decade to 2007. Given the long-term importance of agriculture, it is interesting to note that to date only 0.08% of known land plant species and 0.0002% of known land animal species have been domesticated, compared with 0.17% of known marine plant species and 0.13% of known marine animal species. Domestication typically involves about a decade of scientific research.<sup id="cite_ref-DuarteMarbaHolmer_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DuarteMarbaHolmer-29">[29]</a></sup> Domesticating aquatic species involves fewer risks to humans than do land animals, which took a large toll in human lives. Most major human diseases originated in domesticated animals,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> including diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diphtheria" title="Diphtheria">diphtheria</a>, that like most infectious diseases, move to humans from animals. No human <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a> of comparable virulence have yet emerged from marine species.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p>Biological control methods to manage parasites are already being used such as cleaner fish (e.g. lumpsuckers and wrasse) to control sea lice populations in salmon farming.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Models are being used to help with spatial planning and siting of fish farms in order to minimize impact.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The decline in wild fish stocks has increased the demand for farmed fish.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> However, it is necessary to find alternative sources of protein and oil for fish feed so the aquaculture industry can grow sustainably; otherwise it represents a great risk for the over-exploitation of forage fish.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Another recent issue following the banning in 2008 of organotins by the IMO (International Maritime Organization) is the need to find environmentally friendly, but still effective, compounds with antifouling effects.</p> 
     <p>Many new natural compounds are discovered every year, but it is almost impossible to produce them on a large enough scale for commercial purposes.</p> 
     <p>It’s highly probable that future developments in this field will rely on microorganisms, but greater funding and further research is needed to overcome the lack of knowledge in this field.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Species_groups">Species groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Species groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tmulti tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px">

     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aquatic_plants">Aquatic plants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Aquatic plants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Yangzhuanghe_-_CIMG3404.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Yangzhuanghe_-_CIMG3404.JPG/220px-Yangzhuanghe_-_CIMG3404.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Yangzhuanghe_-_CIMG3404.JPG/330px-Yangzhuanghe_-_CIMG3404.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Yangzhuanghe_-_CIMG3404.JPG/440px-Yangzhuanghe_-_CIMG3404.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Yangzhuanghe_-_CIMG3404.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Cultivating emergent aquatic plants in floating containers
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Algaculture" title="Algaculture">Algaculture</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Seaweed_farming" title="Seaweed farming">Seaweed farming</a>
     </div> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Microalgae" title="Microalgae" class="mw-redirect">Microalgae</a>, also referred to as <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microphytes" title="Microphytes" class="mw-redirect">microphytes</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Planktonic_algae" title="Planktonic algae" class="mw-redirect">planktonic algae</a> constitute the majority of cultivated <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>.</p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Macroalgae" title="Macroalgae" class="mw-redirect">Macroalgae</a>, commonly known as <a href="/wiki/Seaweed" title="Seaweed">seaweed</a>, also have many commercial and industrial uses, but due to their size and specific requirements, they are not easily cultivated on a large scale and are most often taken in the wild.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fish">Fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Fish_farming" title="Fish farming">Fish farming</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The farming of fish is the most common form of aquaculture. It involves raising fish commercially in tanks, ponds, or ocean enclosures, usually for food. A facility that releases juvenile fish into the wild for recreational <a href="/wiki/Fishing" title="Fishing">fishing</a> or to supplement a species' natural numbers is generally referred to as a fish <a href="/wiki/Hatchery" title="Hatchery">hatchery</a>. Worldwide, the most important fish species used in fish farming are, in order, <a href="/wiki/Carp" title="Carp">carp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tilapia" title="Tilapia">tilapia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Catfish" title="Catfish">catfish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-faostat_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faostat-1">[1]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In the Mediterranean, young <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_bluefin_tuna" title="Atlantic bluefin tuna">bluefin tuna</a> are netted at sea and towed slowly towards the shore. They are then interned in <a href="/wiki/Offshore_aquaculture" title="Offshore aquaculture">offshore</a> pens where they are further grown for the market.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> In 2009, researchers in <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> managed for the first time to coax tuna (<a href="/wiki/Southern_bluefin_tuna" title="Southern bluefin tuna">Southern bluefin</a>) to breed in landlocked tanks. <a href="/wiki/Southern_bluefin_tuna" title="Southern bluefin tuna">Southern bluefin tuna</a> are also caught in the wild and fattened in grow-out sea cages in southern <a href="/wiki/Spencer_Gulf" title="Spencer Gulf">Spencer Gulf</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Australia" title="South Australia">South Australia</a>.</p> 
     <p>A similar process is used in the salmon farming section of this industry; juveniles are taken from hatcheries and a variety of methods are used to aid them in their maturation. For example, as stated above, one of the most important fish species in the industry, the salmon, can be grown using a cage system. This is done by having netted cages, preferably in open water that has a strong flow, and feeding the salmon a special food mixture that will aid in their growth. This process allows for year-round growth of the fish, and thus a higher harvest during the correct seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crustaceans">Crustaceans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Crustaceans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Shrimp_farm" title="Shrimp farm" class="mw-redirect">Shrimp farm</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Freshwater_prawn_farm" title="Freshwater prawn farm" class="mw-redirect">Freshwater prawn farm</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Commercial <a href="/wiki/Shrimp" title="Shrimp">shrimp</a> farming began in the 1970s, and production grew steeply thereafter. Global production reached more than 1.6 million tonnes in 2003, worth about 9 billion <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">U.S. dollars</a>. About 75% of farmed shrimp is produced in <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, in particular in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>. The other 25% is produced mainly in <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> is the largest producer. Thailand is the largest exporter.</p> 
     <p>Shrimp farming has changed from its traditional, small-scale form in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> into a global industry. Technological advances have led to ever higher densities per unit area, and <a href="/wiki/Broodstock" title="Broodstock">broodstock</a> is shipped worldwide. Virtually all farmed shrimp are <a href="/wiki/Penaeid" title="Penaeid" class="mw-redirect">penaeids</a> (i.e., shrimp of the <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Penaeidae" title="Penaeidae">Penaeidae</a></i>), and just two species of shrimp, the <a href="/wiki/Whiteleg_shrimp" title="Whiteleg shrimp">Pacific white shrimp</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Giant_tiger_prawn" title="Giant tiger prawn" class="mw-redirect">giant tiger prawn</a>, account for about 80% of all farmed shrimp. These industrial <a href="/wiki/Monoculture" title="Monoculture">monocultures</a> are very susceptible to <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">disease</a>, which has decimated shrimp populations across entire regions. Increasing <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecological</a> problems, repeated disease outbreaks, and pressure and criticism from both <a href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organization" title="Non-governmental organization">NGOs</a> and consumer countries led to changes in the industry in the late 1990s and generally stronger regulations. In 1999, governments, industry representatives, and environmental organizations initiated a program aimed at developing and promoting more <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_agriculture" title="Sustainable agriculture">sustainable farming</a> practices through the <a href="/wiki/Seafood_Watch" title="Seafood Watch">Seafood Watch</a> program.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Freshwater_prawn_farm" title="Freshwater prawn farm" class="mw-redirect">Freshwater prawn farming</a> shares many characteristics with, including many problems with, marine <a href="/wiki/Shrimp_farm" title="Shrimp farm" class="mw-redirect">shrimp farming</a>. Unique problems are introduced by the developmental life cycle of the main species, the <a href="/wiki/Giant_river_prawn" title="Giant river prawn" class="mw-redirect">giant river prawn</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-freshwater_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-freshwater-40">[40]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The global annual production of freshwater prawns (excluding <a href="/wiki/Crayfish" title="Crayfish">crayfish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crab" title="Crab">crabs</a>) in 2003 was about 280,000 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> of which China produced 180,000 tonnes followed by India and Thailand with 35,000 tonnes each. Additionally, China produced about 370,000 tonnes of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_river_crab" title="Chinese river crab" class="mw-redirect">Chinese river crab</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-figis_fresh_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-figis_fresh-41">[41]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Molluscs">Molluscs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Molluscs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Abalone-farm1web.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Abalone-farm1web.jpg/220px-Abalone-farm1web.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Abalone-farm1web.jpg/330px-Abalone-farm1web.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Abalone-farm1web.jpg/440px-Abalone-farm1web.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="525"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Abalone-farm1web.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Abalone farm
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Oyster_farming" title="Oyster farming">Oyster farming</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Geoduck_aquaculture" title="Geoduck aquaculture">Geoduck aquaculture</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Aquacultured shellfish include various <a href="/wiki/Oyster" title="Oyster">oyster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mussel" title="Mussel">mussel</a> and clam species. These bivalves are filter and/or deposit feeders, which rely on ambient primary production rather than inputs of fish or other feed. As such shellfish aquaculture is generally perceived as benign or even beneficial.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Depending on the species and local conditions, bivalve molluscs are either grown on the beach, on longlines, or suspended from rafts and harvested by hand or by dredging. <a href="/wiki/Abalone" title="Abalone">Abalone</a> farming began in the late 1950s and early 1960s in Japan and China.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Since the mid-1990s, this industry has become increasingly successful.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Over-fishing and <a href="/wiki/Poaching" title="Poaching">poaching</a> have reduced wild populations to the extent that farmed abalone now supplies most abalone meat. Sustainably farmed molluscs can be certified by <a href="/wiki/Seafood_Watch" title="Seafood Watch">Seafood Watch</a> and other organizations, including the <a href="/wiki/World_Wildlife_Fund" title="World Wildlife Fund" class="mw-redirect">World Wildlife Fund</a> (WWF). WWF initiated the "Aquaculture Dialogues" in 2004 to develop measurable and performance-based standards for responsibly farmed seafood. In 2009, WWF co-founded the <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture_Stewardship_Council" title="Aquaculture Stewardship Council">Aquaculture Stewardship Council</a> with the Dutch Sustainable Trade Initiative (IDH) to manage the global standards and certification programs.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_groups">Other groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Other groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Other groups include aquatic reptiles, amphibians, and miscellaneous invertebrates, such as <a href="/wiki/Echinoderm" title="Echinoderm">echinoderms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">jellyfish</a>. They are separately graphed at the top right of this section, since they do not contribute enough volume to show clearly on the main graph.</p> 
     <p>Commercially harvested echinoderms include <a href="/wiki/Sea_cucumber" title="Sea cucumber">sea cucumbers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sea_urchin" title="Sea urchin">sea urchins</a>. In China, <a href="/wiki/Sea_cucumber" title="Sea cucumber">sea cucumbers</a> are farmed in artificial ponds as large as 1,000 acres (400&nbsp;ha).<sup id="cite_ref-NF_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NF-46">[46]</a></sup></p> 
     <div style="clear:both;"></div> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Around_the_world">Around the world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Around the world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tmulti tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px"> 
       <div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:">
        Global aquaculture production in million tonnes, 1950–2010, as reported by the FAO 
        <sup id="cite_ref-faostat_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faostat-1">[1]</a></sup>
       </div> 
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px"> 
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Global_aquaculture_production_by_country.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Global_aquaculture_production_by_country.png/300px-Global_aquaculture_production_by_country.png" width="300" height="192" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Global_aquaculture_production_by_country.png/450px-Global_aquaculture_production_by_country.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Global_aquaculture_production_by_country.png/600px-Global_aquaculture_production_by_country.png 2x" data-file-width="1352" data-file-height="865"></a>
        </div> 
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"> 
         <center>
          Main aquaculture countries, 1950–2010
         </center> 
        </div> 
       </div> 
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px"> 
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Global_aquaculture_production_by_country_2010.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Global_aquaculture_production_by_country_2010.png/300px-Global_aquaculture_production_by_country_2010.png" width="300" height="223" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Global_aquaculture_production_by_country_2010.png/450px-Global_aquaculture_production_by_country_2010.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Global_aquaculture_production_by_country_2010.png/600px-Global_aquaculture_production_by_country_2010.png 2x" data-file-width="786" data-file-height="583"></a>
        </div> 
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"> 
         <center>
          Main aquaculture countries in 2010
         </center> 
        </div> 
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture_by_country" title="Aquaculture by country" class="mw-redirect">Aquaculture by country</a>
     </div> 
     <p>In 2012, the total world production of fisheries was 158 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> of which aquaculture contributed 66.6 million tonnes, about 42 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-SOFIA_2014_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SOFIA_2014-47">[47]</a></sup> The growth rate of worldwide aquaculture has been sustained and rapid, averaging about 8 percent per annum for over thirty years, while the take from <a href="/wiki/Wild_fisheries" title="Wild fisheries">wild fisheries</a> has been essentially flat for the last decade. The aquaculture market reached $86 billion<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> in 2009. <sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Aquaculture is an especially important economic activity in China. Between 1980 and 1997, the Chinese Bureau of Fisheries reports, aquaculture harvests grew at an annual rate of 16.7 percent, jumping from 1.9 million tonnes to nearly 23 million tonnes. In 2005, China accounted for 70% of world production.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Aquaculture is also currently one of the fastest growing areas of food production in the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-providence1_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-providence1-2">[2]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Approximately 90% of all U.S. shrimp consumption is farmed and imported.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> In recent years <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a> aquaculture has become a major export in southern <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, especially in <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Montt" title="Puerto Montt">Puerto Montt</a>, Chile's fastest-growing city.</p> 
     <p>A <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">United Nations</a> report titled <i>The State of the World Fisheries and Aquaculture</i> released in May 2014 maintained fisheries and aquaculture support the livelihoods of some 60 million people in <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Aquaculture2014_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aquaculture2014-53">[53]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_laws.2C_regulations.2C_and_management">National laws, regulations, and management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: National laws, regulations, and management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Laws governing aquaculture practices vary greatly by country<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> and are often not closely regulated or easily traceable. In the United States, land-based and nearshore aquaculture is regulated at the <a href="/wiki/National_Aquaculture_Act_of_1980" title="National Aquaculture Act of 1980">federal</a> and state levels;<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> however, there are no national laws governing <a href="/wiki/Offshore_aquaculture" title="Offshore aquaculture">offshore aquaculture</a> in U.S. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://oceanservice.noaa.gov/facts/eez.html">exclusive economic zone</a> waters. In June 2011, the Department of Commerce and <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration</a> released national aquaculture policies<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> to address this issue and "to meet the growing demand for healthy seafood, to create jobs in coastal communities, and restore vital ecosystems." In 2011, <a href="/wiki/Lois_Capps" title="Lois Capps">Congresswoman Lois Capps</a> introduced the <i>National Sustainable Offshore Aquaculture Act of 2011</i><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> "to establish a regulatory system and research program for sustainable offshore aquaculture in the United States exclusive economic zone;" however, the bill was not enacted into law.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Over_reporting">Over reporting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Over reporting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <p>China overwhelmingly dominates the world in reported aquaculture output,<sup id="cite_ref-ChinaStats_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChinaStats-58">[58]</a></sup> reporting a total output which is double that of the rest of the world put together. However, there are issues with the accuracy of China's returns.</p> 
     <p>In 2001, the fisheries scientists Reg Watson and <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Pauly" title="Daniel Pauly">Daniel Pauly</a> expressed concerns in a letter to <i>Nature</i>, that China was over reporting its catch from wild fisheries in the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-overreporting_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-overreporting-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> They said that made it appear that the global catch since 1988 was increasing annually by 300,000 tonnes, whereas it was really shrinking annually by 350,000 tonnes. Watson and Pauly suggested this may be related to China policies where state entities that monitor the economy are also tasked with increasing output. Also, until recently, the promotion of Chinese officials was based on production increases from their own areas.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>China disputes this claim. The official <a href="/wiki/Xinhua_News_Agency" title="Xinhua News Agency">Xinhua News Agency</a> quoted Yang Jian, director general of the Agriculture Ministry's Bureau of Fisheries, as saying that China's figures were "basically correct".<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> However, the <a href="/wiki/FAO" title="FAO" class="mw-redirect">FAO</a> accepts there are issues with the reliability of China's statistical returns, and currently treats data from China, including the aquaculture data, apart from the rest of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p> 
     <div style="clear:both;"></div> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aquacultural_Methods">Aquacultural Methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Aquacultural Methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mariculture">Mariculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Mariculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Fish_farming_in_High_Island,_Hong_Kong.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Fish_farming_in_High_Island%2C_Hong_Kong.jpg/220px-Fish_farming_in_High_Island%2C_Hong_Kong.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Fish_farming_in_High_Island%2C_Hong_Kong.jpg/330px-Fish_farming_in_High_Island%2C_Hong_Kong.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Fish_farming_in_High_Island%2C_Hong_Kong.jpg/440px-Fish_farming_in_High_Island%2C_Hong_Kong.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944"></a>
