     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Mariculture" title="Mariculture">Mariculture</a>
     </div> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Mariculture" title="Mariculture">Mariculture</a> refers to the cultivation of marine organisms in <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">seawater</a>, usually in sheltered coastal waters. The farming of marine fish is an example of mariculture, and so also is the farming of marine crustaceans (such as <a href="/wiki/Shrimp" title="Shrimp">shrimps</a>), molluscs (such as <a href="/wiki/Oyster" title="Oyster">oysters</a>) and seaweed.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Integrated">Integrated</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Integrated">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Integrated_Multi-trophic_Aquaculture" title="Integrated Multi-trophic Aquaculture" class="mw-redirect">Integrated Multi-trophic Aquaculture</a>
     </div> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Integrated_Multi-Trophic_Aquaculture" title="Integrated Multi-Trophic Aquaculture" class="mw-redirect">Integrated Multi-Trophic Aquaculture</a> (IMTA) is a practice in which the by-products (wastes) from one species are recycled to become inputs (<a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">food</a>) for another. Fed aquaculture (for example, <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shrimp" title="Shrimp">shrimp</a>) is combined with inorganic extractive and organic extractive (for example, <a href="/wiki/Shellfish" title="Shellfish">shellfish</a>) aquaculture to create balanced systems for environmental sustainability (biomitigation), economic stability (product diversification and risk reduction) and social acceptability (better management practices).<sup id="cite_ref-Chopin_et_al._2001_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chopin_et_al._2001-65">[65]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>"Multi-Trophic" refers to the incorporation of <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> from different <a href="/wiki/Trophic_level" title="Trophic level">trophic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nutritional" title="Nutritional" class="mw-redirect">nutritional</a> levels in the same system.<sup id="cite_ref-Chopin_2006_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chopin_2006-66">[66]</a></sup> This is one potential distinction from the age-old practice of aquatic <a href="/wiki/Polyculture" title="Polyculture">polyculture</a>, which could simply be the co-culture of different fish species from the same trophic level. In this case, these organisms may all share the same biological and chemical processes, with few <a href="/wiki/Synergy" title="Synergy">synergistic</a> benefits, which could potentially lead to significant shifts in the <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a>. Some traditional polyculture systems may, in fact, incorporate a greater diversity of species, occupying several <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">niches</a>, as extensive cultures (low intensity, low management) within the same pond. The "Integrated" in IMTA refers to the more intensive cultivation of the different species in proximity of each other, connected by nutrient and energy transfer through water.</p> 
     <p>Ideally, the biological and chemical processes in an IMTA system should balance. This is achieved through the appropriate selection and proportions of different species providing different ecosystem functions. The co-cultured species are typically more than just <a href="/wiki/Biofilters" title="Biofilters" class="mw-redirect">biofilters</a>; they are harvestable crops of commercial value.<sup id="cite_ref-Chopin_2006_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chopin_2006-66">[66]</a></sup> A working IMTA system can result in greater total production based on mutual benefits to the co-cultured species and improved <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_health" title="Ecosystem health">ecosystem health</a>, even if the production of individual species is lower than in a <a href="/wiki/Monoculture" title="Monoculture">monoculture</a> over a short term period.<sup id="cite_ref-Neori_et_al._2004_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neori_et_al._2004-67">[67]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Sometimes the term "Integrated Aquaculture" is used to describe the integration of monocultures through water transfer.<sup id="cite_ref-Neori_et_al._2004_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neori_et_al._2004-67">[67]</a></sup> For all intents and purposes however, the terms "IMTA" and "integrated aquaculture" differ only in their degree of descriptiveness. <a href="/wiki/Aquaponics" title="Aquaponics">Aquaponics</a>, fractionated aquaculture, IAAS (integrated agriculture-aquaculture systems), IPUAS (integrated peri-urban-aquaculture systems), and IFAS (integrated fisheries-aquaculture systems) are other variations of the IMTA concept.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Netting_materials">Netting materials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Netting materials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <p>Various materials, including <a href="/wiki/Nylon" title="Nylon">nylon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polyester" title="Polyester">polyester</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polypropylene" title="Polypropylene">polypropylene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polyethylene" title="Polyethylene">polyethylene</a>, plastic-coated welded <a href="/wiki/Wire" title="Wire">wire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rubber" title="Rubber" class="mw-redirect">rubber</a>, patented <a href="/wiki/Rope" title="Rope">rope</a> products (Spectra, Thorn-D, Dyneema), <a href="/wiki/Galvanized_steel" title="Galvanized steel" class="mw-redirect">galvanized steel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> are used for netting in aquaculture fish enclosures around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Braithwaite_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Braithwaite-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> All of these materials are selected for a variety of reasons, including design feasibility, <a href="/wiki/Material_strength" title="Material strength" class="mw-redirect">material strength</a>, cost, and <a href="/wiki/Corrosion_resistance" title="Corrosion resistance" class="mw-redirect">corrosion resistance</a>.</p> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Copper_alloys_in_aquaculture" title="Copper alloys in aquaculture">Copper alloys in aquaculture</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Recently, copper alloys have become important netting materials in aquaculture because they are antimicrobial (i.e., they destroy <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viruses" title="Viruses" class="mw-redirect">viruses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi" class="mw-redirect">fungi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Microbes" title="Microbes" class="mw-redirect">microbes</a>) and they therefore prevent <a href="/wiki/Biofouling" title="Biofouling">biofouling</a> (i.e., the undesirable accumulation, adhesion, and growth of microorganisms, plants, algae, tubeworms, barnacles, mollusks, and other organisms). By inhibiting microbial growth, copper alloy aquaculture cages avoid costly net changes that are necessary with other materials. The resistance of organism growth on copper alloy nets also provides a cleaner and healthier environment for farmed fish to grow and thrive.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Issues">Issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture_of_salmon#Issues" title="Aquaculture of salmon" class="mw-redirect">Issues with salmon aquaculture</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Aquaculture can be more environmentally damaging than exploiting <a href="/wiki/Wild_fisheries_of_the_world" title="Wild fisheries of the world" class="mw-redirect">wild fisheries</a> on a local area basis but has considerably less impact on the global environment on a per kg of production basis.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Local concerns include waste handling, side-effects of <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic" class="mw-redirect">antibiotics</a>, competition between farmed and wild animals, and using other fish to feed more marketable <a href="/wiki/Carnivorous" title="Carnivorous" class="mw-redirect">carnivorous</a> fish. However, research and commercial feed improvements during the 1990s and 2000s have lessened many of these concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Aquaculture may contribute to propagation of <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasive species</a>. As the cases of <a href="/wiki/Nile_perch" title="Nile perch">Nile perch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Janitor_fish" title="Janitor fish" class="mw-redirect">Janitor fish</a> show, this issue may be damaging to native fauna.</p> 
     <p>Fish waste is organic and composed of nutrients necessary in all components of aquatic food webs. In-ocean aquaculture often produces much higher<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> than normal fish waste concentrations. The waste collects on the ocean bottom, damaging or eliminating bottom-dwelling life. Waste can also decrease <a href="/wiki/Dissolved_oxygen" title="Dissolved oxygen" class="mw-redirect">dissolved oxygen</a> levels in the <a href="/wiki/Water_column" title="Water column">water column</a>, putting further pressure on wild animals.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fish_oils">Fish oils</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Fish oils">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      Further information: 
      <a href="/wiki/Tilapia#Nutrition" title="Tilapia">Tilapia § Nutrition</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Tilapia from aquaculture has been shown to contain more fat and a much higher ratio of omega-6 to omega-3 oils.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impacts_on_wild_fish">Impacts on wild fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Impacts on wild fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Some carnivorous and omnivorous farmed fish species are fed wild <a href="/wiki/Forage_fish" title="Forage fish">forage fish</a>. Although carnivorous farmed fish represented only 13 percent of aquaculture production by weight in 2000, they represented 34 percent of aquaculture production by value.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Farming of carnivorous species like salmon and shrimp leads to a high demand for forage fish to match the nutrition they get in the wild. Fish do not actually produce omega-3 fatty acids, but instead accumulate them from either consuming <a href="/wiki/Microalgae" title="Microalgae" class="mw-redirect">microalgae</a> that produce these fatty acids, as is the case with forage fish like <a href="/wiki/Herring" title="Herring">herring</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sardines" title="Sardines" class="mw-redirect">sardines</a>, or, as is the case with fatty <a href="/wiki/Predatory_fish" title="Predatory fish">predatory fish</a>, like salmon, by eating <a href="/wiki/Prey_fish" title="Prey fish" class="mw-redirect">prey fish</a> that have accumulated <a href="/wiki/Omega-3_fatty_acid" title="Omega-3 fatty acid">omega-3 fatty acids</a> from microalgae. To satisfy this requirement, more than 50 percent of the world <a href="/wiki/Fish_oil" title="Fish oil">fish oil</a> production is fed to farmed salmon.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Farmed salmon consume more <a href="/wiki/Wild_fish" title="Wild fish" class="mw-redirect">wild fish</a> than they generate as a final product, although the efficiency of production is improving. To produce one pound of farmed salmon, products from several pounds of wild fish are fed to them - this can be described as the "fish-in-fish-out" (FIFO) ratio. In 1995, salmon had a FIFO ratio of 7.5 (meaning 7.5 pounds of wild fish feed were required to produce 1 pound of salmon); by 2006 the ratio had fallen to 4.9.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Additionally, a growing share of fish oil and fishmeal come from residues (byproducts of fish processing), rather than dedicated whole fish. In 2012, 34 percent of fish oil and 28 percent of fishmeal came from residues.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> However, fishmeal and oil from residues instead of whole fish have a different composition with more ash and less protein, which may limit its potential use for aquaculture.</p> 
     <p>As the salmon farming industry expands, it requires more wild forage fish for feed, at a time when seventy five percent of the worlds monitored fisheries are already near to or have exceeded their <a href="/wiki/Maximum_sustainable_yield" title="Maximum sustainable yield">maximum sustainable yield</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Alliance_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alliance-9">[9]</a></sup> The industrial scale extraction of wild forage fish for salmon farming then impacts the survivability of the wild predator fish who rely on them for food. An important step in reducing the impact of aquaculture on wild fish is shifting carnivorous species to plant-based feeds. Salmon feeds, for example, have gone from containing only fishmeal and oil to containing 40 percent plant protein.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> The USDA has also experimented with using grain-based feeds for farmed <a href="/wiki/Trout" title="Trout">trout</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> When properly formulated (and often mixed with fishmeal or oil), plant-based feeds can provide proper nutrition and similar growth rates in carnivorous farmed fish.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Another impact aquaculture production can have on wild fish is the risk of fish escaping from coastal pens, where they can interbreed with their wild counterparts, diluting wild genetic stocks.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Escaped fish can become <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasive</a>, out-competing native species.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Natureza_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Natureza-86">[86]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coastal_ecosystems">Coastal ecosystems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Coastal ecosystems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Aquaculture is becoming a significant threat to <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_ecosystem" title="Aquatic ecosystem">coastal ecosystems</a>. About 20 percent of mangrove forests have been destroyed since 1980, partly due to <a href="/wiki/Shrimp_farm" title="Shrimp farm" class="mw-redirect">shrimp farming</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-87">[87]</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Extended_cost" title="Extended cost">extended cost</a>–benefit analysis of the <a href="/wiki/Total_Economic_Value" title="Total Economic Value" class="mw-redirect">total economic value</a> of shrimp aquaculture built on mangrove ecosystems found that the <a href="/wiki/Externality" title="Externality">external</a> costs were much higher than the external benefits.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Over four decades, 269,000 hectares (660,000 acres) of Indonesian mangroves have been converted to shrimp farms. Most of these farms are abandoned within a decade because of the <a href="/wiki/Toxin" title="Toxin">toxin</a> build-up and <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrient</a> loss.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Aquaculture_of_salmon" title="Aquaculture of salmon" class="mw-redirect">Salmon farms</a> are typically sited in pristine coastal ecosystems which they then pollute. A farm with 200,000 salmon discharges more fecal waste than a city of 60,000 people. This waste is discharged directly into the surrounding aquatic environment, untreated, often containing antibiotics and <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-Alliance_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alliance-9">[9]</a></sup> There is also an accumulation of <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metals" title="Heavy metals" class="mw-redirect">heavy metals</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Benthos" title="Benthos">benthos</a> (seafloor) near the salmon farms, particularly <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_coho_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_coho-91">[91]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_modification">Genetic modification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Genetic modification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>A type of salmon called the AquAdvantage salmon has been <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified" title="Genetically modified" class="mw-redirect">genetically modified</a> for faster growth, although it has not been approved for commercial use, due to controversy.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> The altered salmon incorporates a growth hormone from a <a href="/wiki/Chinook_salmon" title="Chinook salmon">Chinook salmon</a> that allows it to reach full size in 16-28 months, instead of the normal 36 months for Atlantic salmon, and while consuming 25 percent less feed.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> The U.S. Food and Drug Administration reviewed the AquAdvantage salmon in a draft environmental assessment and determined that it "would not have a significant impact (FONSI) on the U.S. environment."<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_welfare">Animal welfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Animal welfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Pain_in_fish" title="Pain in fish">Pain in fish</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Pain_in_invertebrates" title="Pain in invertebrates">Pain in invertebrates</a>
     </div> 
     <p>As with the farming of terrestrial animals, social attitudes influence the need for humane practices and regulations in farmed marine animals. Under the guidelines advised by the <a href="/wiki/Farm_Animal_Welfare_Council" title="Farm Animal Welfare Council" class="mw-redirect">Farm Animal Welfare Council</a> good animal welfare means both fitness and a sense of well being in the animal's physical and mental state. This can be defined by the <a href="/wiki/Five_Freedoms" title="Five Freedoms" class="mw-redirect">Five Freedoms</a>:</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>Freedom from hunger &amp; thirst</li> 
      <li>Freedom from discomfort</li> 
      <li>Freedom from pain, disease, or injury</li> 
      <li>Freedom to express normal behaviour</li> 
      <li>Freedom from fear and distress</li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>However, the controversial issue in aquaculture is whether fish and farmed marine invertebrates are actually <a href="/wiki/Sentient" title="Sentient" class="mw-redirect">sentient</a>, or have the perception and awareness to experience suffering. Although no evidence of this has been found in marine invertebrates,<sup id="cite_ref-Hastein_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hastein-95">[95]</a></sup> recent studies conclude that fish do have the necessary receptors (<a href="/wiki/Nociceptors" title="Nociceptors" class="mw-redirect">nociceptors</a>) to sense noxious stimuli and so are likely to experience states of pain, fear and stress.<sup id="cite_ref-Hastein_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hastein-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Consequently, welfare in aquaculture is directed at vertebrates; finfish in particular.<sup id="cite_ref-Conte_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conte-97">[97]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Common_welfare_concerns">Common welfare concerns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Common welfare concerns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Welfare in aquaculture can be impacted by a number of issues such as stocking densities, behavioural interactions, <a href="/wiki/Fish_diseases_and_parasites#Disease" title="Fish diseases and parasites">disease</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fish_diseases_and_parasites#Parasites" title="Fish diseases and parasites">parasitism</a>. A major problem in determining the cause of impaired welfare is that these issues are often all interrelated and influence each other at different times.<sup id="cite_ref-Huntingford_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huntingford-98">[98]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Optimal stocking density is often defined by the <a href="/wiki/Carrying_capacity" title="Carrying capacity">carrying capacity</a> of the stocked environment and the amount of individual space needed by the fish, which is very species specific. Although behavioural interactions such as <a href="/wiki/Shoaling_and_schooling" title="Shoaling and schooling">shoaling</a> may mean that high stocking densities are beneficial to some species,<sup id="cite_ref-Hastein_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hastein-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ashley_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ashley-99">[99]</a></sup> in many cultured species high stocking densities may be of concern. Crowding can constrain normal swimming behaviour, as well as increase aggressive and competitive behaviours such as cannibalism,<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> feed competition,<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> territoriality and dominance/subordination hierarchies.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis-102">[102]</a></sup> This potentially increases the risk of tissue damage due to abrasion from fish-to-fish contact or fish-to-cage contact.<sup id="cite_ref-Hastein_95-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hastein-95">[95]</a></sup> Fish can suffer reductions in food intake and <a href="/wiki/Feed_conversion_ratio" title="Feed conversion ratio">food conversion efficiency</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis-102">[102]</a></sup> In addition, high stocking densities can result in water flow being insufficient, creating inadequate oxygen supply and waste product removal.<sup id="cite_ref-Ashley_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ashley-99">[99]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dissolved_oxygen" title="Dissolved oxygen" class="mw-redirect">Dissolved oxygen</a> is essential for fish respiration and concentrations below critical levels can induce stress and even lead to <a href="/wiki/Asphyxiation" title="Asphyxiation" class="mw-redirect">asphyxiation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis_102-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis-102">[102]</a></sup> Ammonia, a nitrogen excretion product, is highly toxic to fish at accumulated levels, particularly when oxygen concentrations are low.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Many of these interactions and effects cause stress in the fish, which can be a major factor in facilitating fish disease.<sup id="cite_ref-Conte_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conte-97">[97]</a></sup> For many parasites, infestation depends on the host's degree of mobility, the density of the host population and vulnerability of the host's defence system.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Sea lice are the primary parasitic problem for finfish in aquaculture, high numbers causing widespread skin erosion and haemorrhaging, gill congestion,and increased mucus production.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> There are also a number of prominent viral and bacterial <a href="/wiki/Pathogens" title="Pathogens" class="mw-redirect">pathogens</a> that can have severe effects on internal organs and nervous systems.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Improving_welfare">Improving welfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Improving welfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>The key to improving welfare of marine cultured organisms is to reduce stress to a minimum, as prolonged or repeated stress can cause a range of adverse effects. Attempts to minimise stress can occur throughout the culture process. During grow out it is important to keep stocking densities at appropriate levels specific to each species, as well as separating size classes and grading to reduce aggressive behavioural interactions. Keeping nets and cages clean can assist positive water flow to reduce the risk of water degradation.</p> 
     <p>Not surprisingly disease and parasitism can have a major effect on fish welfare and it is important for farmers not only to manage infected stock but also to apply disease prevention measures. However, prevention methods, such as vaccination, can also induce stress because of the extra handling and injection.<sup id="cite_ref-Ashley_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ashley-99">[99]</a></sup> Other methods include adding antibiotics to feed, adding chemicals into water for treatment baths and biological control, such as using <a href="/wiki/Cleaner_fish" title="Cleaner fish">cleaner wrasse</a> to remove lice from farmed salmon.<sup id="cite_ref-Ashley_99-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ashley-99">[99]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Many steps are involved in transport, including capture, food deprivation to reduce faecal contamination of transport water, transfer to transport vehicle via nets or pumps, plus transport and transfer to the delivery location. During transport water needs to be maintained to a high quality, with regulated temperature, sufficient oxygen and minimal waste products.<sup id="cite_ref-Conte_97-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conte-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ashley_99-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ashley-99">[99]</a></sup> In some cases <a href="/wiki/Anaesthetics" title="Anaesthetics" class="mw-redirect">anaesthetics</a> may be used in small doses to calm fish before transport.<sup id="cite_ref-Ashley_99-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ashley-99">[99]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Aquaculture is sometimes part of an environmental rehabilitation program or as an aid in conserving endangered species.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prospects">Prospects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Prospects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <p>Global <a href="/wiki/Wild_fisheries" title="Wild fisheries">wild fisheries</a> are in decline, with valuable habitat such as <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuaries</a> in critical condition.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The aquaculture or <a href="/wiki/Fish_farm" title="Fish farm" class="mw-redirect">farming</a> of <a href="/wiki/Piscivorous" title="Piscivorous" class="mw-redirect">piscivorous</a> fish, like <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a>, does not help the problem because they need to eat products from other fish, such as <a href="/wiki/Fish_meal" title="Fish meal">fish meal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fish_oil" title="Fish oil">fish oil</a>. Studies have shown that <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture_of_salmon" title="Aquaculture of salmon" class="mw-redirect">salmon farming</a> has major <a href="/wiki/Salmon_farming_issues" title="Salmon farming issues" class="mw-redirect">negative impacts</a> on wild salmon, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Forage_fish" title="Forage fish">forage fish</a> that need to be caught to feed them.<sup id="cite_ref-Salmon_Run_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salmon_Run-109">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Fish that are higher on the <a href="/wiki/Trophic_level" title="Trophic level">food chain</a> are less efficient sources of food energy.</p> 
     <p>Apart from fish and shrimp, some aquaculture undertakings, such as seaweed and filter-feeding bivalve mollusks like <a href="/wiki/Oyster" title="Oyster">oysters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clam" title="Clam">clams</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mussel" title="Mussel">mussels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scallop" title="Scallop">scallops</a>, are relatively benign and even environmentally restorative.<sup id="cite_ref-uscnews.usc.edu_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uscnews.usc.edu-26">[26]</a></sup> Filter-feeders filter pollutants as well as nutrients from the water, improving water quality.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Seaweeds" title="Seaweeds" class="mw-redirect">Seaweeds</a> extract nutrients such as inorganic nitrogen and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> directly from the water,<sup id="cite_ref-Chopin_et_al._2001_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chopin_et_al._2001-65">[65]</a></sup> and filter-feeding <a href="/wiki/Mollusk" title="Mollusk" class="mw-redirect">mollusks</a> can extract nutrients as they feed on particulates, such as <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Detritus" title="Detritus">detritus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rice_2008_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rice_2008-112">[112]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Some profitable aquaculture cooperatives promote sustainable practices.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> New methods lessen the risk of biological and chemical <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a> through minimizing fish stress, fallowing netpens, and applying <a href="/wiki/Integrated_Pest_Management" title="Integrated Pest Management" class="mw-redirect">Integrated Pest Management</a>. <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">Vaccines</a> are being used more and more to reduce <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic" class="mw-redirect">antibiotic</a> use for disease control.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Onshore recirculating aquaculture systems, facilities using <a href="/wiki/Polyculture" title="Polyculture">polyculture</a> techniques, and properly sited facilities (for example, offshore areas with strong currents) are examples of ways to manage negative environmental effects.</p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Recirculating_aquaculture_systems" title="Recirculating aquaculture systems" class="mw-redirect">Recirculating aquaculture systems</a> (RAS) recycle water by circulating it through filters to remove fish waste and food and then recirculating it back into the tanks. This saves water and the waste gathered can be used in <a href="/wiki/Compost" title="Compost">compost</a> or, in some cases, could even be treated and used on land. While RAS was developed with freshwater fish in mind, scientist associated with the <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_Research_Service" title="Agricultural Research Service">Agricultural Research Service</a> have found a way to rear saltwater fish using RAS in low-salinity waters.<sup id="cite_ref-Growing_Premium_Seafood-Inland.21_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Growing_Premium_Seafood-Inland.21-115">[115]</a></sup> Although saltwater fish are raised in off-shore cages or caught with nets in water that typically has a salinity of 35 <a href="/wiki/Parts_per_thousand" title="Parts per thousand" class="mw-redirect">parts per thousand</a> (ppt), scientists were able to produce healthy pompano, a saltwater fish, in tanks with a salinity of only 5 ppt. Commercializing low-salinity RAS are predicted to have positive environmental and economical effects. Unwanted nutrients from the fish food would not be added to the ocean and the risk of transmitting diseases between wild and farm-raised fish would greatly be reduced. The price of expensive saltwater fish, such as the pompano and combia used in the experiments, would be reduced. However, before any of this can be done researchers must study every aspect of the fish's lifecycle, including the amount of ammonia and nitrate the fish will tolerate in the water, what to feed the fish during each stage of its lifecycle, the <a href="/wiki/Stocking_rate" title="Stocking rate" class="mw-redirect">stocking rate</a> that will produce the healthiest fish, etc.<sup id="cite_ref-Growing_Premium_Seafood-Inland.21_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Growing_Premium_Seafood-Inland.21-115">[115]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Some 16 countries now use <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">geothermal energy</a> for aquaculture, including China, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, and the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-earth-policy_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-earth-policy-116">[116]</a></sup> In California, for example, 15 fish farms produce tilapia, bass, and catfish with warm water from underground. This warmer water enables fish to grow all year round and mature more quickly. Collectively these California farms produce 4.5 million kilograms of fish each year.<sup id="cite_ref-earth-policy_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-earth-policy-116">[116]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aquaculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
